city)">Akrotiri</a>, the island of Thera, communities and agricultural areas on nearby islands, and the coast of <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> with related <a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">earthquakes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunamis</a>.</td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header" style="background:#F6DA9F"></th></tr><tr><td .pr>div{background:white!important;color:#000!important}html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data .locmap div{background:transparent!important}}</style><div class="locmap" style="width:250px;float:right;clear:right"><div style="width:250px;padding:0"><div style="position:relative;width:250px"><span class="notpageimage" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Greece_relief_location_map.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Thera is located in Greece"><img alt="Thera is located in Greece" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Greece_relief_location_map.jpg/250px-Greece_relief_location_map.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="206" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Greece_relief_location_map.jpg/375px-Greece_relief_location_map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d1/Greece_relief_location_map.jpg/500px-Greece_relief_location_map.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1003" data-file-height="825" /></a></span><div class="od notheme" style="top:75.541%;left:58.333%;font-size:91%"><div class="id" style="left:-4px;top:-4px"><span class="notpageimage" typeof="mw:File"><span title="Thera"><img alt="Thera" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Red_pog.svg/8px-Red_pog.svg.png" decoding="async" width="8" height="8" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Red_pog.svg/12px-Red_pog.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Red_pog.svg/16px-Red_pog.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="64" data-file-height="64" /></span></span></div><div class="pr" style="width:6em;left:5px"><div>Thera</div></div></div></div></div></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The <b>Minoan eruption</b> was a catastrophic <a href="/wiki/Types_of_volcanic_eruptions" title="Types of volcanic eruptions">volcanic eruption</a> that devastated the <a href="/wiki/Aegean_Islands" title="Aegean Islands">Aegean island</a> of <a href="/wiki/Santorini" title="Santorini">Thera (also called Santorini)</a> circa 1600 <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">BCE</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It destroyed the <a href="/wiki/Akrotiri_(prehistoric_city)" title="Akrotiri (prehistoric city)">Minoan settlement at Akrotiri</a>, as well as communities and agricultural areas on nearby islands and the coast of <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> with subsequent <a href="/wiki/Earthquake" title="Earthquake">earthquakes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paleotsunami" title="Paleotsunami">paleotsunamis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Antonopoulos_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Antonopoulos-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> With a <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_Explosivity_Index" class="mw-redirect" title="Volcanic Explosivity Index">Volcanic Explosivity Index</a> (VEI) of 7, it resulted in the ejection of approximately 28–41&#160;km<sup>3</sup> (6.7–9.8&#160;cu&#160;mi) of <a href="/wiki/Dense-rock_equivalent" title="Dense-rock equivalent">dense-rock equivalent</a> (DRE),<sup id="cite_ref-:0_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:13_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the eruption was <a href="/wiki/List_of_large_Holocene_volcanic_eruptions" title="List of large Holocene volcanic eruptions">one of the largest volcanic events in human history</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Oppenheimer2003_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oppenheimer2003-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McCoy2002_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCoy2002-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sigurdsson_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sigurdsson-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since <a href="/wiki/Tephra" title="Tephra">tephra</a> from the Minoan eruption serves as a <a href="/wiki/Marker_horizon" title="Marker horizon">marker horizon</a> in nearly all <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_site" title="Archaeological site">archaeological sites</a> in the Eastern Mediterranean,<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> its precise date is of high importance and has been fiercely debated among archaeologists and <a href="/wiki/Volcanologist" title="Volcanologist">volcanologists</a> for decades,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> without coming to a definite conclusion.
</p><p>Although there are no clear ancient records of the eruption, its <a href="/wiki/Eruption_column" title="Eruption column">plume</a> and <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_lightning" title="Volcanic lightning">volcanic lightning</a> may have been described in the Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Tempest_Stele" title="Tempest Stele">Tempest Stele</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Chinese <i><a href="/wiki/Bamboo_Annals" title="Bamboo Annals">Bamboo Annals</a></i> reported unusual yellow skies and summer frost at the beginning of the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>, which may have been a consequence of <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_winter" title="Volcanic winter">volcanic winter</a> (similar to 1816, the <a href="/wiki/Year_Without_a_Summer" title="Year Without a Summer">Year Without a Summer</a>, after the <a href="/wiki/1815_eruption_of_Mount_Tambora" title="1815 eruption of Mount Tambora">1815 eruption of Mount Tambora</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-PangEtAl1989_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PangEtAl1989-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Eruption">Eruption</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Eruption"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:010607-0930-17_-_Nea_Kameni_-_Krater.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Small figures of people can be seen at the top looking into craters strewn with grey rocks." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/010607-0930-17_-_Nea_Kameni_-_Krater.jpg/220px-010607-0930-17_-_Nea_Kameni_-_Krater.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/010607-0930-17_-_Nea_Kameni_-_Krater.jpg/330px-010607-0930-17_-_Nea_Kameni_-_Krater.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/010607-0930-17_-_Nea_Kameni_-_Krater.jpg/440px-010607-0930-17_-_Nea_Kameni_-_Krater.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a><figcaption>Volcanic craters on Santorini, June 2001</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Background">Background</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Background"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1236090951">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}@media print{body.ns-0 .mw-parser-output .hatnote{display:none!important}}</style><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Santorini_caldera" title="Santorini caldera">Santorini caldera</a></div>
<p>Geological evidence shows the Thera volcano erupted numerous times over several hundred thousand years before the Minoan eruption. In a repeating process, the volcano would violently erupt, then eventually collapse into a roughly circular seawater-filled <a href="/wiki/Caldera" title="Caldera">caldera</a>, with numerous small islands forming the circle. The caldera would slowly refill with magma, building a new volcano, which erupted and then collapsed in an ongoing cyclical process.<sup id="cite_ref-Friedrich_Book_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Friedrich_Book-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Immediately before the Minoan eruption, the walls of the caldera formed a nearly continuous ring of islands, with the only entrance between Thera and the tiny island of <a href="/wiki/Aspronisi" title="Aspronisi">Aspronisi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Friedrich_Book_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Friedrich_Book-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This cataclysmic eruption was centered on a small island just north of the existing island of <a href="/wiki/Nea_Kameni" title="Nea Kameni">Nea Kameni</a> in the centre of the then-existing caldera. The northern part of the caldera was refilled by the <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_ash" title="Volcanic ash">volcanic ash</a> and lava, then collapsed again.
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Magnitude">Magnitude</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Magnitude"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The magnitude of the eruption, particularly the submarine pyroclastic flows, has been difficult to estimate because the majority of the erupted products were deposited in the sea. Together, these challenges result in considerable uncertainty regarding the volume of the Minoan eruption, with estimates ranging between 13–86&#160;km<sup>3</sup> (3.1–20.6&#160;cu&#160;mi) DRE.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>According to the latest analysis of marine sediments and seismic data gathered during ocean research expeditions from 2015 to 2019, the estimated volume of the material expelled during the volcanic eruption ranges from 28–41&#160;km<sup>3</sup> (6.7–9.8&#160;cu&#160;mi) DRE.<sup id="cite_ref-:13_1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The study revealed that the initial Plinian eruption was the most voluminous phase, ejecting 14–21&#160;km<sup>3</sup> (3.4–5.0&#160;cu&#160;mi) magma and accounting for half of total erupted materials. This was followed by 3–4&#160;km<sup>3</sup> (0.72–0.96&#160;cu&#160;mi) DRE co-ignimbrite fall, 5–9&#160;km<sup>3</sup> (1.2–2.2&#160;cu&#160;mi) DRE pyroclastic flows and 5–7&#160;km<sup>3</sup> (1.2–1.7&#160;cu&#160;mi) DRE intra-caldera deposits.<sup id="cite_ref-:13_1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:13-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>This eruption is comparable with the <a href="/wiki/1815_eruption_of_Mount_Tambora" title="1815 eruption of Mount Tambora">1815 eruption of Mount Tambora</a>, <a href="/wiki/1257_Samalas_eruption" title="1257 Samalas eruption">1257 Samalas eruption</a>, Lake Taupo's <a href="/wiki/Hatepe_eruption" title="Hatepe eruption">Hatepe eruption</a> around 230&#160;CE, and the <a href="/wiki/946_eruption_of_Paektu_Mountain" title="946 eruption of Paektu Mountain">946 eruption of Paektu Mountain</a>, which are among the largest eruptions during the <a href="/wiki/Common_Era" title="Common Era">Common Era</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Oppenheimer2003_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Oppenheimer2003-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McCoy2002_7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCoy2002-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Sequence">Sequence</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Sequence"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>On Santorini, there is a 60&#160;m (200&#160;ft) thick layer of white <a href="/wiki/Tephra" title="Tephra">tephra</a> that overlies the soil clearly delineating the ground level before the eruption. This layer has three distinct bands that indicate the different phases of the eruption.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Studies have identified four major eruption phases, and one minor precursory tephra fall. The thinness of the first ash layer, along with the lack of noticeable erosion of that layer by winter rains before the next layer was deposited, indicate that the volcano gave the local population a few months' warning. Since no human remains have been found at the Akrotiri site, this preliminary volcanic activity probably caused the island's population to flee. It is also suggested that several months before the eruption, Santorini experienced one or more earthquakes, which damaged the local settlements.<sup id="cite_ref-McCoy_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCoy-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Sivertsen_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sivertsen-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Akrotiri-Santorini_volcanic-pumice_Aegean-Sea_Greece.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Akrotiri-Santorini_volcanic-pumice_Aegean-Sea_Greece.jpg/170px-Akrotiri-Santorini_volcanic-pumice_Aegean-Sea_Greece.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="255" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Akrotiri-Santorini_volcanic-pumice_Aegean-Sea_Greece.jpg/255px-Akrotiri-Santorini_volcanic-pumice_Aegean-Sea_Greece.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Akrotiri-Santorini_volcanic-pumice_Aegean-Sea_Greece.jpg/340px-Akrotiri-Santorini_volcanic-pumice_Aegean-Sea_Greece.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="1800" /></a><figcaption>Early phase of Late-Bronze-Age volcano eruption (~ 1500 BC), southern border of the Caldera island. The lower layer of <a href="/wiki/Pumice" title="Pumice">pumice</a> is finer, almost white and without rock intrusions. </figcaption></figure>
<p>Intense magmatic activity of the first major phase (BO<sub>1</sub>/Minoan A)<sup id="cite_ref-McCoyHeiken_21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCoyHeiken-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> of the eruption deposited up to 7&#160;m (23&#160;ft) of <a href="/wiki/Pumice" title="Pumice">pumice</a> and ash, with a minor lithic component, southeast and east. Archaeological evidence indicated burial of man-made structures with limited damage. The second (BO<sub>2</sub>/Minoan B) and third (BO<sub>3</sub>/Minoan C) eruption phases involved <a href="/wiki/Pyroclastic_surge" title="Pyroclastic surge">pyroclastic surges</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lava_fountain" class="mw-redirect" title="Lava fountain">lava fountaining</a>, as well as the possible generation of <a href="/wiki/Tsunami" title="Tsunami">tsunamis</a>. Man-made structures not buried during Minoan A were completely destroyed. The third phase was also characterized by the initiation of caldera collapse. The fourth, and last, major phase (BO<sub>4</sub>/Minoan D) was marked by varied activity: lithic-rich base surge deposits, <a href="/wiki/Lava" title="Lava">lava</a> flows, <a href="/wiki/Lahar" title="Lahar">lahar</a> floods, and co-<a href="/wiki/Ignimbrite" title="Ignimbrite">ignimbrite</a> ash-fall deposits. This phase was characterized by the completion of caldera collapse, which produced <a href="/wiki/Megatsunamis" class="mw-redirect" title="Megatsunamis">megatsunamis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-McCoyHeiken_21-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McCoyHeiken-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Geomorphology">Geomorphology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Geomorphology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Santoryn-Ia_4.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Enclave of structures built into the side of a steep cliff. Swimming pools are visible." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Santoryn-Ia_4.jpg/170px-Santoryn-Ia_4.jpg" decoding="async" width="170" height="227" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Santoryn-Ia_4.jpg/255px-Santoryn-Ia_4.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Santoryn-Ia_4.jpg/340px-Santoryn-Ia_4.jpg 2x" data-file-width="461" data-file-height="615" /></a><figcaption>Mansions and hotels atop steep cliffs.</figcaption></figure>
<p>Although the fracturing process is not yet known, the altitudinal statistical analysis indicates that the caldera had formed just before the eruption. The area of the island was smaller, and the southern and eastern coastlines appeared regressed. During the eruption, the landscape was covered by the pumice sediments. In some places, the coastline vanished under thick <a href="/wiki/Tuff" title="Tuff">tuff</a> depositions. In others, recent coastlines were extended towards the sea. After the eruption, the geomorphology of the island was characterized by an intense erosional phase during which the pumice was progressively removed from the higher altitudes to the lower ones.<sup id="cite_ref-Gournelos_23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gournelos-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Volcanology">Volcanology</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Volcanology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The eruption was of the <a href="/wiki/Plinian_eruption" title="Plinian eruption">Ultra Plinian type</a>, and it resulted in an estimated 30 to 35&#160;km (19 to 22&#160;mi) high <a href="/wiki/Eruption_column" title="Eruption column">eruption column</a> which reached the <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a>. In addition, the <a href="/wiki/Magma" title="Magma">magma</a> underlying the volcano came into contact with the shallow marine <a href="/wiki/Embayment" class="mw-redirect" title="Embayment">embayment</a>, resulting in violent <a href="/wiki/Phreatomagmatic_eruption" title="Phreatomagmatic eruption">phreatomagmatic blasts</a>.
</p><p>The eruption also generated 35 to 150&#160;m (115 to 492&#160;ft) high tsunamis that devastated the northern coastline of Crete, 110&#160;km (68&#160;mi) away. The tsunami affected coastal towns such as <a href="/wiki/Amnisos" title="Amnisos">Amnisos</a>, where building walls were knocked out of alignment. On the island of <a href="/wiki/Anafi" title="Anafi">Anafi</a>, 27&#160;km (17&#160;mi) to the east, ash layers 3&#160;m (10&#160;ft) deep have been found, as well as pumice layers on slopes 250&#160;m (820&#160;ft) <a href="/wiki/Above_mean_sea_level" class="mw-redirect" title="Above mean sea level">above sea level</a>.
</p><p>Elsewhere in the Mediterranean are pumice deposits that could have been sent by the Thera eruption. Ash layers in cores drilled from the seabed and from lakes in <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a> show that the heaviest ashfall was towards the east and northeast of Santorini. The ash found on Crete is now known to have been from a precursory phase of the eruption, some weeks or months before the main eruptive phases, and it would have had little impact on the island.<sup id="cite_ref-Keenan_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keenan-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Santorini ash deposits were at one time claimed to have been found in the <a href="/wiki/Nile_Delta" title="Nile Delta">Nile Delta</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but this is now known to be a misidentification.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Eruption_dating">Eruption dating</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Eruption dating"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The Minoan eruption is an important <a href="/wiki/Marker_horizon" title="Marker horizon">marker horizon</a> for the <a href="/wiki/Bronze_Age" title="Bronze Age">Bronze Age</a> chronology of the Eastern Mediterranean realm. It provides a fixed point for aligning the entire chronology of the second millennium BCE in the Aegean, as evidence of the eruption is found throughout the region. Yet, archaeological dating based on <a href="/wiki/Sequence_dating" title="Sequence dating">typological sequencing</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_chronology" title="Egyptian chronology">Egyptian chronology</a> is significantly younger than the radiocarbon age of the Minoan eruption, by roughly a century. This age discrepancy has resulted in a fierce debate about whether there is an upheaval in the archaeological synchronization between the Aegean and Egypt.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Archaeology">Archaeology</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Archaeology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Archaeologists developed the Late Bronze Age chronologies of eastern <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Sea" title="Mediterranean Sea">Mediterranean</a> cultures by analyzing <a href="/wiki/Style_(visual_arts)#Style_in_archaeology" title="Style (visual arts)">design styles</a> of artifacts found in each archaeological layer.<sup id="cite_ref-Warren_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Warren-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> If the type of artifacts can be accurately assigned, then the layer's position in a chronological order can be determined. This is known as <a href="/wiki/Sequence_dating" title="Sequence dating">sequence dating</a> or <a href="/wiki/Seriation_(archaeology)" title="Seriation (archaeology)">seriation</a>. In Aegean chronology, however, the frequent exchange of objects and styles enables relative chronology to be compared with the absolute chronology of Egypt, so absolute dates could be determined in the Aegean.
</p><p>Since the Minoan eruption has been conclusively placed in late/end <a href="/wiki/Minoan_chronology" title="Minoan chronology">Late Minoan IA</a> (LM-IA) in the Crete chronology, late/end <a href="/wiki/Helladic_chronology" title="Helladic chronology">Late Helladic I</a> (LH-I) in the mainland chronology,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> the contention concerns which Egyptian period was contemporaneous with LM-IA and LM-IB. Decades of intensive archaeological work and <a href="/wiki/Seriation_(archaeology)" title="Seriation (archaeology)">seriation</a> on Crete in the last century had confidently correlated the late LM-IA with <a href="/wiki/Eighteenth_Dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Eighteenth Dynasty of Egypt">Dynasty XVIII</a> in Egypt and the end of LM-IA at the start of <a href="/wiki/Thutmose_III" title="Thutmose III">Thutmose III</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_31-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Stone vessels discovered in the <a href="/wiki/Shaft_tomb" title="Shaft tomb">Shaft Graves</a> in LH-I are also of the <a href="/wiki/New_Kingdom_of_Egypt" title="New Kingdom of Egypt">New Kingdom</a> type. Multiple archaeological sites of Theran pumice workshop used by the local inhabitants are only found in the New Kingdom strata. A milk bowl on Santorini used before the volcanic eruption has a New Kingdom pottery style.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_28-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An Egyptian inscription on the <a href="/wiki/Tempest_Stele" title="Tempest Stele">Ahmose Tempest Stele</a> recorded an extraordinary cataclysm resembling the Minoan eruption.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Taken together, the archaeological evidence points to an eruption date after the accession of <a href="/wiki/Ahmose_I" title="Ahmose I">Ahmose I</a>. The year of accession based on the conventional Egyptian chronology and radiocarbon-based chronology is either 1550 BCE<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and 1570–1544 BCE (IntCal04)<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> or 1569–1548 BCE (IntCal20).<sup id="cite_ref-:3_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The archaeological evidence argues for a Theran eruption date between circa 1550 and 1480 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Proponents of an earlier date argue that Aegean-Egyptian pottery correlation allows considerable flexibility. Several other archaeological interpretations of LM-IA and LM-IB pottery differ from the "traditional" and could be consistent with a much earlier beginning time for LM-IA and LM-IB.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:4_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Pottery synchronisms were also assessed to be less secure before the <a href="/wiki/Amenhotep_III" title="Amenhotep III">LM-IIIAI/Amenhotep III</a> period.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Pumice in workshop and the inscription on the Tempest Stele have been argued to only reflect the <a href="/wiki/Terminus_post_quem" title="Terminus post quem">lower bound</a> of the eruption age. The date of the production of pottery with the Santorini milk bowl style in other regions has not been determined and could pre-date the Minoan eruption. The chronology of stone vessel styles during this critical period is lacking.<sup id="cite_ref-:5_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:5-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Radiocarbon_age">Radiocarbon age</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Radiocarbon age"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Before_present" class="mw-redirect" title="Before present">Raw radiocarbon dates</a> are not accurate calendar years of the event and this has to do with the fact that the level of atmospheric radiocarbon fluctuates. Raw radiocarbon ages can be converted to calendar dates by means of <a href="/wiki/Radiocarbon_calibration" title="Radiocarbon calibration">calibration curves</a> which are periodically updated by international researchers. Derived calibrated calendar date ranges are highly dependent on how accurately the calibration curve represents radiocarbon levels for the time period. As of 2022, the most updated calibration curve is IntCal20.<sup id="cite_ref-:6_44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:6-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Early radiocarbon dates in the 1970s with calibration were already showing massive age disagreement and were initially discarded as unreliable by the archaeological community.<sup id="cite_ref-:4_39-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:4-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the following decades, the range of possible eruption dates narrowed significantly with improved calibration, analytical precision, statistical methods, and sample treatment. Radiocarbon dating has built a strong case for an eruption date in the late 17th century BCE. The table below summarizes the history and results of radiocarbon dating of volcanic <a href="/wiki/Destruction_layer" title="Destruction layer">destruction layer</a> with pre-2018 calibration curves:
</p>

<p>In 2018, a team led by tree ring scientist reported a possible offset of a few decades in the previous IntCal calibration curves during the period 1660–1540 BCE. The resulting new calibration curve allowed previous raw radiocarbon dates to be calibrated to encompass a substantial part of the 16th century BCE, making it possible for radiocarbon dates to be compatible with archaeological evidence.<sup id="cite_ref-Pearson2018_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pearson2018-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The measured offset was then confirmed by other laboratories across the world and incorporated into the most updated calibration curve IntCal20.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In the same year, study of <a href="/wiki/Bomb_pulse" title="Bomb pulse">bomb peak</a> further questioned the validity of wiggle-matching of the olive branch because the radiocarbon dates of the outermost branch layer could differ by up to a few decades caused by growth cessation, then the olive branch could also pre-date Thera by decades.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In 2020, speculation of regional offset specific to Mediterranean context in all calibration curves was reported based on measurements made on juniper wood at <a href="/wiki/Gordion" title="Gordion">Gordion</a>. If the regional offset is genuine, then calibration based on the regional dataset, Hd GOR, would place the eruption date back to 17th century BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-:10_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:10-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Others have argued that these site-specific offsets are already incorporated into the IntCal20 prediction interval since it is constructed from a much wider range of locations and any locational variation is of similar magnitude to the inter-laboratory variation.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>While the refined calibration curve IntCal20 does not rule out a 17th-century BCE eruption date, it does shift the probable range of the eruption date to include the majority of 16th century BCE, offering a way to at least mitigate the long-standing age disagreement. However, the exact year of eruption has not been settled. The table below summarizes the dating results:
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Ice_cores,_tree_rings_and_speleothems"><span id="Ice_cores.2C_tree_rings_and_speleothems"></span>Ice cores, tree rings and speleothems</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Ice cores, tree rings and speleothems"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>An eruption of Theran magnitude is expected to leave a detectable signal in various environmental records like <a href="/wiki/Ice_core" title="Ice core">ice core</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dendroclimatology" title="Dendroclimatology">tree ring</a>. Petrologic constraints on Minoan magma yields a range of 0.3–35.9 trillion grams of sulfur release. The higher end of the estimate could cause severe <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_winter" title="Volcanic winter">climatic change</a> and leave detectable signals in ice cores and tree rings.<sup id="cite_ref-67" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Notably, <a href="/wiki/Dendrochronology" title="Dendrochronology">tree ring dating</a> allows extremely precise dating to the exact calendar year of each ring with virtually no age uncertainty, and from properties of the annual tree rings <a href="/wiki/Dendroclimatology" title="Dendroclimatology">local climate record could be reconstructed</a> down to sub-annual precision.
</p><p>In 1987, a major Greenland sulfate spike in 1644 ± 20 BCE in ice core chronology was hypothesized to be caused by the Minoan eruption based on the early radiocarbon results of Hammer et al.<sup id="cite_ref-:7_45-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:7-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1988, a major environmental disruption and extreme global-cooling/frost-ring in 1627 ± 0 BCE were also revealed through precisely dated <a href="/wiki/Frost_ring" class="mw-redirect" title="Frost ring">frost ring</a> and too were hypothesized to be related to Minoan eruption.<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Archaeologists who preferred a late 16th century BCE eruption date were neither convinced by the 1644 ± 20 BCE sulfate spike nor by the 1627 BCE frost ring, because evidence of causality between the two events and Minoan eruption was absent.<sup id="cite_ref-:2_31-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Since 2003, multiple independent studies of major elements and trace elements of volcanic ash retrieved from the 1644 ± 20 BCE sulfate layer failed to match the ash to that of Santorini<sup id="cite_ref-Keenan_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Keenan-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> but all attributed the ash to another large eruption during this period, <a href="/wiki/Mount_Aniakchak" title="Mount Aniakchak">Mount Aniakchak</a>, thus ruling out Minoan eruption as the cause of the sulfate spike.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pearson_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pearson-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2019, a revision of the Greenland ice-core chronology was proposed based on synchronization of the frost-ring data and the major sulfate spike, and the revised date for the Aniakchak eruption was shifted to 1628 BCE.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Greenland ice core chronology offset was independently confirmed by other teams<sup id="cite_ref-Pearson_74-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pearson-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and adopted into Greenland Ice Core Chronology 2021 (GICC21).<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The 1627 BCE extreme global cooling was then conveniently explained by the major Aniakchak eruption without invoking Thera. An eruption date of 1627 BCE is also no longer supported by radiocarbon evidence with the most recent calibration curve IntCal20.<sup id="cite_ref-Pearson_74-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pearson-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In light of much younger radiocarbon dates and revised ice core chronology, several possible ice core and tree ring signals in the 17th and 16th century BCE have been proposed.<sup id="cite_ref-Pearson_74-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pearson-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:12_79-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:12-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The list below summarizes the tree ring and ice core signals that may have been caused by the Minoan eruption:
</p>

<p>The date of Minoan eruption does not necessarily have to be in one of the years listed in the table, because the eruption may not have been environmentally impactful enough to leave any detectable signal.<sup id="cite_ref-:11_65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:11-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In addition, a <a href="/wiki/Stalagmite" title="Stalagmite">stalagmite</a> from Turkey shows bromine peaks at 1621 ± 25 BCE, molybdenum at 1617 ± 25 BCE and sulfur at 1589 ± 25 BCE. The authors interpreted that all three peaks were caused by a single volcanic eruption in the Mediterranean region and the time difference was related to differences in their retention rates.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Others have suggested that the sulfur peak may have been related to the 1561 BCE chemical anomaly recorded in Mediterranean tree rings.<sup id="cite_ref-Pearson2018_54-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pearson2018-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Historical_impact">Historical impact</h2><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Historical impact"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Akrotiri">Akrotiri</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Akrotiri"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Akrotiri-greece-feve.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Excavation into rock showing doors and windows among the rubble." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Akrotiri-greece-feve.jpg/220px-Akrotiri-greece-feve.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="148" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Akrotiri-greece-feve.jpg/330px-Akrotiri-greece-feve.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/51/Akrotiri-greece-feve.jpg/440px-Akrotiri-greece-feve.jpg 2x" data-file-width="579" data-file-height="390" /></a><figcaption>Excavation of Akrotiri on Thera</figcaption></figure> 
<p>The eruption devastated the settlement at <a href="/wiki/Akrotiri_(prehistoric_city)" title="Akrotiri (prehistoric city)">Akrotiri</a> on Santorini, which was entombed in a layer of <a href="/wiki/Pumice" title="Pumice">pumice</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ash" title="Ash">ash</a>. Evidence at the site suggests that survivors returned and attempted to recover their possessions and perhaps to bury victims.<sup id="cite_ref-DoumasHandbook_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DoumasHandbook-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Minoan_Crete">Minoan Crete</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Minoan Crete"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<figure typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:AMI_-_Oktopusvase.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="A Minoan vase featuring an octopus." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/AMI_-_Oktopusvase.jpg/225px-AMI_-_Oktopusvase.jpg" decoding="async" width="225" height="266" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/AMI_-_Oktopusvase.jpg/338px-AMI_-_Oktopusvase.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1e/AMI_-_Oktopusvase.jpg/450px-AMI_-_Oktopusvase.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1840" data-file-height="2176" /></a><figcaption>A <i>Marine Style</i> vase, typical of the Late Minoan IB period that followed the eruption of Thera.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The eruption was felt at <a href="/wiki/Minoan_civilization" title="Minoan civilization">Minoan</a> sites on Crete. In northeastern Crete, earthquakes destroyed sites including <a href="/wiki/Petras" title="Petras">Petras</a>, while 9 meter high tsunamis swept over coastal sites such as <a href="/wiki/Palaikastro" title="Palaikastro">Palaikastro</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-YoungerRehak_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YoungerRehak-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ash and pumice fell across the island, where it was sometimes collected and stored.<sup id="cite_ref-YoungerRehak_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YoungerRehak-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MacGSackHandbook_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacGSackHandbook-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>After the eruption, the Minoans quickly recovered, and the subsequent period is considered the zenith of Minoan culture.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-HallagerHandbook_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HallagerHandbook-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Many affected sites were rebuilt, including Petras and Palaikastro, at the latter of which, new buildings were constructed using high quality <a href="/wiki/Ashlar_masonry" class="mw-redirect" title="Ashlar masonry">ashlar</a> masonry. New <a href="/wiki/Minoan_palaces" title="Minoan palaces">Minoan palaces</a> were constructed at <a href="/wiki/Zakros" title="Zakros">Zakros</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phaistos" title="Phaistos">Phaistos</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-MacGSackHandbook_86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MacGSackHandbook-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, other sites fell into decline, including <a href="/wiki/Galatas_Palace" title="Galatas Palace">Galatas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kommos_(Crete)" title="Kommos (Crete)">Kommos</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HallagerHandbook_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HallagerHandbook-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-YoungerRehak_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-YoungerRehak-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p><p>The longer term impact of the eruption remains a matter of debate. The immediate aftermath saw a number of puzzling cultural changes including the filling in of <a href="/wiki/Lustral_basin" title="Lustral basin">lustral basins</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-HallagerHandbook_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HallagerHandbook-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In their book <i>The Troubled Island</i>, Driessen and MacDonald argued that the richness of the post-eruption material culture masked deep economic and political problems that eventually led to the collapse of Neopalatial society. Subsequent evidence suggests that this was not a general pattern across the island.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Chinese_records">Chinese records</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Chinese records"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>A volcanic winter from an eruption in the late 17th century BCE has been claimed by some researchers to correlate with entries in later Chinese records documenting the collapse of the semi-legendary <a href="/wiki/Xia_dynasty" title="Xia dynasty">Xia dynasty</a> in <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>. According to the <i><a href="/wiki/Bamboo_Annals" title="Bamboo Annals">Bamboo Annals</a></i>, the collapse of the dynasty and the rise of the <a href="/wiki/Shang_dynasty" title="Shang dynasty">Shang dynasty</a>, approximately dated to 1618 BCE, were accompanied by "yellow fog, a dim sun, then <a href="/wiki/Sun_dogs" class="mw-redirect" title="Sun dogs">three suns</a>, frost in July, famine, and the withering of all <a href="/wiki/Five_Grains" title="Five Grains">five cereals</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-PangEtAl1989_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PangEtAl1989-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Effect_on_Egyptian_history">Effect on Egyptian history</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Effect on Egyptian history"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Ahmose_I#Dates_and_length_of_reign" title="Ahmose I">Ahmose I §&#160;Dates and length of reign</a></div>
<p>Apocalyptic rainstorms, which devastated much of <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a>, and were described on the Tempest Stele of <a href="/wiki/Ahmose_I" title="Ahmose I">Ahmose I</a>, have been attributed to short-term climatic changes caused by the Theran eruption.<sup id="cite_ref-Foster_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Foster-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Davis_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Davis-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-96" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The dates and regnal dates of Ahmose I are in some dispute with Egyptologists (leaving aside alternate chronologies). Proposed reigns range from 1570 to 1546&#160;BCE to 1539–1514&#160;BCE. A radiocarbon dating of his mummy produced a mean value of 1557 BCE. In any case this would only provide an overlap with the later estimates of eruption date.<sup id="cite_ref-97" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Alternatively, if the eruption occurred in the <a href="/wiki/Second_Intermediate_Period_of_Egypt" title="Second Intermediate Period of Egypt">Second Intermediate Period</a>, the absence of Egyptian records of the eruption could be caused by the general disorder in Egypt around that time.
</p><p>While it has been argued that the damage attributed to these storms may have been caused by an earthquake following the Thera eruption, it has also been suggested that it was caused during a war with the <a href="/wiki/Hyksos" title="Hyksos">Hyksos</a>, and the storm reference is merely a metaphor for chaos upon which the Pharaoh was attempting to impose order.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiener_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiener-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Documents such as <a href="/wiki/Hatshepsut" title="Hatshepsut">Hatshepsut</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Speos_Artemidos" title="Speos Artemidos">Speos Artemidos</a> depict storms, but are clearly figurative, not literal. Research indicates that the Speos Artemidos stele is a reference to her overcoming the powers of chaos and darkness.<sup id="cite_ref-Wiener_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wiener-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Greek_traditions">Greek traditions</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Greek traditions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Deucalion#Dating_by_early_scholars" title="Deucalion">Deucalion §&#160;Dating by early scholars</a></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="The_Titanomachy">The Titanomachy</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: The Titanomachy"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>The eruption of Thera and volcanic fallout may have inspired the myths of the <a href="/wiki/Titanomachy" title="Titanomachy">Titanomachy</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Theogony" title="Theogony">Theogony</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-Greene_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Greene-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The Titanomachy could have picked up elements of western <a href="/wiki/Anatolia" title="Anatolia">Anatolian</a> folk memory, as the tale spread westward. Hesiod's lines have been compared with volcanic activity, citing <a href="/wiki/Zeus" title="Zeus">Zeus</a>'s thunderbolts as <a href="/wiki/Volcanic_lightning" title="Volcanic lightning">volcanic lightning</a>, the boiling earth and sea as a breach of the <a href="/wiki/Magma_chamber" title="Magma chamber">magma chamber</a>, immense flame and heat as evidence of <a href="/wiki/Phreatic_explosion" class="mw-redirect" title="Phreatic explosion">phreatic explosions</a>, among many other descriptions.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Atlantis">Atlantis</h4><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Atlantis"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1236090951"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Location_hypotheses_of_Atlantis" title="Location hypotheses of Atlantis">Location hypotheses of Atlantis</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Spyridon_Marinatos" title="Spyridon Marinatos">Spyridon Marinatos</a>, the discoverer of the Akrotiri archaeological site, suggested that the Minoan eruption is reflected in <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>'s story of <a href="/wiki/Atlantis" title="Atlantis">Atlantis</a>. This view remains prevalent in popular culture, as reflected in TV programs such as BBC's <i><a href="/wiki/Atlantis_(2011_film)" title="Atlantis (2011 film)">Atlantis</a></i>. However, this view is not supported by current scholarship.<sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ManningHandbook_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ManningHandbook-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-105" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> 
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="The_Exodus">The Exodus</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: The Exodus"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>Geologist Barbara J. Sivertsen seeks to establish a link between the eruption of Santorini (c. 1600 BCE) and <a href="/wiki/The_Exodus" title="The Exodus">the Exodus</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Israelites" title="Israelites">Israelites</a> from <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Egypt" title="Ancient Egypt">Egypt</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">Bible</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Sivertsen_20-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sivertsen-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Bicameral_mentality">Bicameral mentality</h3><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Minoan_eruption&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Bicameral mentality"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></div>
<p>In the controversial <a href="/wiki/Bicameral_mentality" title="Bicameral mentality">bicameral mentality</a> hypothesis, <a href="/wiki/Julian_Jaynes" title="Julian Jaynes">Julian Jaynes</a> has argued that the Minoan eruption was a crucial event in the development of human <a href="/wiki/Consciousness" title="Consciousness">consciousness</a> since the displacements that it caused led to new and important interactions among communities.<sup id="cite_ref-106" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<li id="cite_note-Oppenheimer2003-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-Oppenheimer2003_6-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-Oppenheimer2003_6-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222"><cite id="CITEREFOppenheimer2003" class="citation journal cs1">Oppenheimer, Clive (2003). <li id="cite_note-ManningHandbook-103"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ManningHandbook_103-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1238218222"><cite id="CITEREFManning2012" class="citation encyclopaedia cs1">Manning, Stuart (2012). Oxford University Press. pp.&#160;<span class="nowrap">457–</span>454. <a href="/wiki/Doi_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="Doi (identifier)">doi</a>:<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://doi.org/10.1093%2Foxfordhb%2F9780199873609.013.0034">10.1093/oxfordhb/9780199873609.013.0034</a>. <a href="/wiki/ISBN_(identifier)" class="mw-redirect" title="ISBN (identifier)">ISBN</a>&#160;<a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0199873609" title="Special:BookSources/978-0199873609"><bdi>978-0199873609</bdi></a>. <q>Marinatos (1939) famously suggested that the eruption might even have caused the destruction of Minoan Crete (also Page 1970). Although this simple hypothesis has been negated by the findings of excavation and other research since the late 1960s... which demonstrate that the eruption occurred late in the Late Minoan IA ceramic period, whereas the destructions of the Cretan palaces and so on are some time subsequent (late in the following Late Minoan IB ceramic period)</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=bookitem&amp;rft.atitle=Eruption+of+Thera%2FSantorini&amp;rft.btitle=The+Oxford+Handbook+of+the+Bronze+Age+Aegean&amp;rft.pages=%3Cspan+class%3D%22nowrap%22%3E457-%3C%2Fspan%3E454&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft.date=2012&amp;rft_id=info%3Adoi%2F10.1093%2Foxfordhb%2F9780199873609.013.0034&amp;rft.isbn=978-0199873609&amp;rft.aulast=Manning&amp;rft.aufirst=Stuart&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AMinoan+eruption" class="Z3988"></span></span>
